physiologist <a href="/wiki/John_Burdon-Sanderson" title="John Burdon-Sanderson">John Burdon-Sanderson</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:230px;max-width:230px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:251px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Man_is_But_a_Worm.jpg"><img alt="Darwin's figure is shown seated, dressed in a toga, in a circular frame labelled &quot;TIME'S METER&quot; around which a succession of figures spiral, starting with an earthworm emerging from the broken letters &quot;CHAOS&quot; then worms with head and limbs, followed by monkeys, apes, primitive men, a loin cloth clad hunter with a club, and a gentleman who tips his top hat to Darwin." data-file-height="1216" data-file-width="1101" decoding="async" height="252" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Man_is_But_a_Worm.jpg/228px-Man_is_But_a_Worm.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Man_is_But_a_Worm.jpg/342px-Man_is_But_a_Worm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Man_is_But_a_Worm.jpg/456px-Man_is_But_a_Worm.jpg 2x" width="228"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><i><a href="/wiki/Punch_(magazine)" title="Punch (magazine)">Punch</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s <a href="/wiki/Almanac" title="Almanac">almanac</a> for 1882, published shortly before Darwin's death, depicts him amidst evolution from chaos to Victorian gentleman with the title <i>Man Is But A Worm</i>.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>His research using images was expanded in his 1872 book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Expression_of_the_Emotions_in_Man_and_Animals" title="The Expression of the Emotions in Man and Animals">The Expression of the Emotions in Man and Animals</a></i>, one of the first books to feature printed photographs, which discussed the <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_psychology" title="Evolutionary psychology">evolution of human psychology</a> and its continuity with the <a href="/wiki/Ethology" title="Ethology">behaviour of animals</a>. Both books proved very popular, and Darwin was impressed by the general assent with which his views had been received, remarking that "everybody is talking about it without being shocked."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> His conclusion was "that man with all his noble qualities, with sympathy which feels for the most debased, with benevolence which extends not only to other men but to the humblest living creature, with his god-like intellect which has penetrated into the movements and constitution of the solar system—with all these exalted powers—Man still bears in his bodily frame the indelible stamp of his lowly origin."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup>
</p><p>His evolution-related experiments and investigations led to books on <i><a href="/wiki/Insectivorous_Plants_(book)" title="Insectivorous Plants (book)">Insectivorous Plants</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Effects_of_Cross_and_Self_Fertilisation_in_the_Vegetable_Kingdom" title="The Effects of Cross and Self Fertilisation in the Vegetable Kingdom">The Effects of Cross and Self Fertilisation in the Vegetable Kingdom</a></i>, different forms of flowers on plants of the same species, and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Power_of_Movement_in_Plants" title="The Power of Movement in Plants">The Power of Movement in Plants</a></i>. He continued to collect information and exchange views from scientific correspondents all over the world, including <a href="/wiki/Mary_Treat" title="Mary Treat">Mary Treat</a>, whom he encouraged to persevere in her scientific work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> He was the first person to recognize the significance of carnivory in plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hedrich_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hedrich-176">[176]</a></sup> His botanical work<sup class="reference" id="ref_Inone"><a href="#endnote_Inone">[IX]</a></sup> was interpreted and popularised by various writers including <a href="/wiki/Grant_Allen" title="Grant Allen">Grant Allen</a> and <a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells" title="H. G. Wells">H. G. Wells</a>, and helped transform plant science in the late 19th century and early 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pain_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pain-177">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Endersby_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-Endersby-178">[178]</a></sup> In his last book he returned to <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Formation_of_Vegetable_Mould_through_the_Action_of_Worms" title="The Formation of Vegetable Mould through the Action of Worms">The Formation of Vegetable Mould through the Action of Worms</a></i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_and_funeral">Death and funeral</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Darwin_from_Insectivorous_Plants_to_Worms" title="Darwin from Insectivorous Plants to Worms">Darwin from Insectivorous Plants to Worms</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Herschel%26darwin.jpg"><img alt="Tombs of John Herschel, left black marble, and Charles Darwin. white marble in Westminster Abbey" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2239" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Herschel%26darwin.jpg/280px-Herschel%26darwin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Herschel%26darwin.jpg/420px-Herschel%26darwin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Herschel%26darwin.jpg/560px-Herschel%26darwin.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Herschel%26darwin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The adjoining tombs of the scientists <a href="/wiki/John_Herschel" title="John Herschel">John Herschel</a> and Charles Darwin in the nave of <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a>, London</div></div></div>
<p>In 1882 he was diagnosed with what was called "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angina_pectoris" title="Angina pectoris">angina pectoris</a>" which then meant <a href="/wiki/Coronary_thrombosis" title="Coronary thrombosis">coronary thrombosis</a> and disease of the heart. At the time of his death, the physicians diagnosed "anginal attacks", and "heart-failure", there has since been speculation about the causes of long-term problems with the <a href="/wiki/Health_of_Charles_Darwin" title="Health of Charles Darwin">health of Charles Darwin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Colp_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-Colp-179">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clayton_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clayton-180">[180]</a></sup>
</p><p>He died at <a href="/wiki/Down_House" title="Down House">Down House</a> on 19 April 1882. His last words were to his family, telling Emma "I am not the least afraid of death—Remember what a good wife you have been to me—Tell all my children to remember how good they have been to me". While she rested, he repeatedly told Henrietta and Francis "It's almost worth while to be sick to be nursed by you".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup>
</p><p>He had expected to be buried in St Mary's churchyard at <a href="/wiki/Downe" title="Downe">Downe</a>, but at the request of Darwin's colleagues, after public and parliamentary petitioning, <a href="/wiki/William_Spottiswoode" title="William Spottiswoode">William Spottiswoode</a> (President of the Royal Society) arranged for Darwin to be honoured by <a href="/wiki/Burials_and_memorials_in_Westminster_Abbey" title="Burials and memorials in Westminster Abbey">burial in Westminster Abbey</a>, close to <a href="/wiki/John_Herschel" title="John Herschel">John Herschel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>. The funeral, held on Wednesday 26 April, was attended by thousands of people, including family, friends, scientists, philosophers and dignitaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Westminster_Abbey_CD_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Westminster_Abbey_CD-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charles_Robert_Darwin_by_John_Collier.jpg"><img alt="Three-quarter portrait of a senior Darwin dressed in black before a black background. His face and six-inch white beard are dramatically lit from the side. His eyes are shaded by his brows and look directly and thoughtfully at the viewer." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3124" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="364" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Charles_Robert_Darwin_by_John_Collier.jpg/280px-Charles_Robert_Darwin_by_John_Collier.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Charles_Robert_Darwin_by_John_Collier.jpg/420px-Charles_Robert_Darwin_by_John_Collier.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Charles_Robert_Darwin_by_John_Collier.jpg/560px-Charles_Robert_Darwin_by_John_Collier.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charles_Robert_Darwin_by_John_Collier.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In 1881, Darwin was an eminent figure, still working on his contributions to evolutionary thought that had an enormous effect on many fields of science. His portrait by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Collier_(Pre-Raphaelite_painter)" title="John Collier (Pre-Raphaelite painter)">John Collier</a> was commissioned for the <a href="/wiki/Linnean_Society_of_London" title="Linnean Society of London">Linnean Society of London</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>As <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Russel_Wallace" title="Alfred Russel Wallace">Alfred Russel Wallace</a> put it, Darwin had "wrought a greater revolution in human thought within a quarter of a century than any man of our time – or perhaps any time", having "given us a new conception of the world of life, and a theory which is itself a powerful instrument of research; has shown us how to combine into one consistent whole the facts accumulated by all the separate classes of workers, and has thereby revolutionised the whole study of nature".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Van_Wyhe_2021_p._155_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-Van_Wyhe_2021_p._155-183">[183]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most scientists were now convinced of <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a> as descent with modification, though few agreed with Darwin that <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a> "has been the main but not the exclusive means of modification".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-main_but_not_exclusive_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-main_but_not_exclusive-184">[184]</a></sup>
During "<a href="/wiki/The_eclipse_of_Darwinism" title="The eclipse of Darwinism">the eclipse of Darwinism</a>" scientists explored alternative mechanisms. Then <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Fisher" title="Ronald Fisher">Ronald Fisher</a> incorporated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mendelian_genetics" title="Mendelian genetics">Mendelian genetics</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Genetical_Theory_of_Natural_Selection" title="The Genetical Theory of Natural Selection">The Genetical Theory of Natural Selection</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> leading to <a href="/wiki/Population_genetics" title="Population genetics">population genetics</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Modern_synthesis_(20th_century)" title="Modern synthesis (20th century)">modern evolutionary synthesis</a>, which continues to develop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-b3847_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-b3847-23">[23]</a></sup> Scientific discoveries have confirmed and validated Darwin's key insights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Van_Wyhe_2021_p._155_183-1"><a href="#cite_note-Van_Wyhe_2021_p._155-183">[183]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Commemoration">Commemoration</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Commemoration_of_Charles_Darwin" title="Commemoration of Charles Darwin">Commemoration of Charles Darwin</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_things_named_after_Charles_Darwin" title="List of things named after Charles Darwin">List of things named after Charles Darwin</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_taxa_described_by_Charles_Darwin" title="List of taxa described by Charles Darwin">List of taxa described by Charles Darwin</a></div>
<p>Geographical features given his name include <a href="/wiki/Darwin_Sound" title="Darwin Sound">Darwin Sound</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Mount_Darwin_(Andes)" title="Mount Darwin (Andes)">Mount Darwin</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> both named while he was on <a href="/wiki/Second_voyage_of_HMS_Beagle" title="Second voyage of HMS Beagle">the <i>Beagle</i> voyage</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Darwin_Harbour" title="Darwin Harbour">Darwin Harbour</a>, named by his former shipmates on <a href="/wiki/HMS_Beagle#Third_voyage_(1837–1843)" title="HMS Beagle">its next voyage</a>, which eventually became the location of <a href="/wiki/Darwin,_Northern_Territory" title="Darwin, Northern Territory">Darwin</a>, the capital city of Australia's <a href="/wiki/Northern_Territory" title="Northern Territory">Northern Territory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NTDoPaI_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-NTDoPaI-188">[188]</a></sup>
Darwin's name was given, <a href="/wiki/Binomial_nomenclature" title="Binomial nomenclature">formally</a> or <a href="/wiki/Common_name" title="Common name">informally</a>, to numerous plants and animals, including many he had collected on the voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup> Some names were given later, for example, <a href="/wiki/Tanager" title="Tanager">tanagers</a> found in the <a href="/wiki/Gal%C3%A1pagos_Islands" title="Galápagos Islands">Galápagos Islands</a> became popularly known as "<a href="/wiki/Darwin%27s_finches" title="Darwin's finches">Darwin's finches</a>" in 1947.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charles_Darwin_(39400069234).jpg"><img alt="Statue of Darwin in Natural History Museum, London" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5472" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Charles_Darwin_%2839400069234%29.jpg/200px-Charles_Darwin_%2839400069234%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Charles_Darwin_%2839400069234%29.jpg/300px-Charles_Darwin_%2839400069234%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Charles_Darwin_%2839400069234%29.jpg/400px-Charles_Darwin_%2839400069234%29.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charles_Darwin_(39400069234).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of Darwin in the <a href="/wiki/Natural_History_Museum,_London" title="Natural History Museum, London">Natural History Museum</a>, London</div></div></div>
<p>In 1883 Sir <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Edgar_Boehm" title="Joseph Edgar Boehm">Joseph Boehm</a> sculpted a white marble life-size seated statue of Darwin. It was installed at the top of the main staircase in the Central Hall of the <a href="/wiki/Natural_History_Museum,_London" title="Natural History Museum, London">Natural History Museum</a> in London, and unveiled on 9 June 1885 by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Henry_Huxley" title="Thomas Henry Huxley">Thomas Huxley</a> in the presence of <a href="/wiki/Edward_VII" title="Edward VII">Edward, Prince of Wales</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Bartholomew_Sulivan" title="Bartholomew Sulivan">Bartholomew Sulivan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Parslow" title="Joseph Parslow">Joseph Parslow</a> also at the ceremony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreeman2007[httpdarwin-onlineorgukcontentframesetitemIDA27bviewtypetextpageseq101_94]_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreeman2007[httpdarwin-onlineorgukcontentframesetitemIDA27bviewtypetextpageseq101_94]-192">[192]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Van_Helvert_Van_Wyhe_2021_115–117_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-Van_Helvert_Van_Wyhe_2021_115–117-193">[193]</a></sup> In 1927 its place was taken by an elephant, and in 1970 it was moved to the North Hall of the museum, then in May 2008 the statue was moved back to its original place, displacing a statue of the Museum's founder <a href="/wiki/Richard_Owen" title="Richard Owen">Richard Owen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Van_Helvert_Van_Wyhe_2021_115–117_193-1"><a href="#cite_note-Van_Helvert_Van_Wyhe_2021_115–117-193">[193]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup>
</p><p>Darwin's children, now eminent, preserved his memory. In 1887 <a href="/wiki/Francis_Darwin" title="Francis Darwin">Francis Darwin</a> published <i><a href="/wiki/The_Life_and_Letters_of_Charles_Darwin" title="The Life and Letters of Charles Darwin">Life and Letters</a></i> with his own reminiscences of his father, an autobiography Darwin had written for his family (edited to spare the sensitivities of Darwin's widow Emma), and correspondence. More volumes of correspondence followed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDesmondMooreBrowne2007111_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDesmondMooreBrowne2007111-195">[195]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Darwin_Statue.jpg"><img alt="Unveiling of the bronze Darwin Statue outside the former Shrewsbury School building in 1897 surrounded by schoolboys in straw hats" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="550" data-file-width="443" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Darwin_Statue.jpg/180px-Darwin_Statue.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Darwin_Statue.jpg/270px-Darwin_Statue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Darwin_Statue.jpg/360px-Darwin_Statue.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Darwin_Statue.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Unveiling in 1897 of the Darwin Statue at the former <a href="/wiki/Shrewsbury_School" title="Shrewsbury School">Shrewsbury School</a> building where he had studied</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Shrewsbury_School" title="Shrewsbury School">Shrewsbury School</a> building, which Darwin attended as a boy, became the <a href="/wiki/Shrewsbury_Library" title="Shrewsbury Library">Free Library</a>. A seated statue of Darwin was installed outside it, and unveiled in 1897.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Van_Helvert_Van_Wyhe_2021_115–117_193-2"><a href="#cite_note-Van_Helvert_Van_Wyhe_2021_115–117-193">[193]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Linnean_Society_of_London" title="Linnean Society of London">Linnean Society of London</a> began awards of the <a href="/wiki/Darwin%E2%80%93Wallace_Medal" title="Darwin–Wallace Medal">Darwin–Wallace Medalin</a> 1908, to mark fifty years from the joint reading on 1 July 1858 of <a href="/wiki/On_the_Tendency_of_Species_to_form_Varieties;_and_on_the_Perpetuation_of_Varieties_and_Species_by_Natural_Means_of_Selection" title="On the Tendency of Species to form Varieties; and on the Perpetuation of Varieties and Species by Natural Means of Selection">papers by Darwin and Wallace publishing their theory</a>. Further awards were made in 1958 and 2008, since 2010 the medal awards have been annual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Linnean_Medal_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-Linnean_Medal-196">[196]</a></sup>
</p><p>In June 1909, more than 400 officials and scientists from across the world met in <a href="/wiki/Cambridge" title="Cambridge">Cambridge</a> to <a href="/wiki/Darwin_Day" title="Darwin Day">commemorate Darwin's centenary</a> and the fiftieth anniversary of <i>On the Origin of Species</i>, with exhibitions and events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-b222_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-b222-197">[197]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Darwin_Centennial_Celebration_(1959)" title="Darwin Centennial Celebration (1959)">Darwin Centennial Celebration</a> in 1959, on the centenary of <i>On the Origin of Species</i> and 150 years from Darwin's birth, had major commemorations at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Chicago" title="University of Chicago">University of Chicago</a>. It featured the <a href="/wiki/Modern_synthesis_(20th_century)" title="Modern synthesis (20th century)">evolutionary synthesis</a>, and claims to Darwin's legacy by biologists with differing views. It was marked by <a href="/wiki/Gavin_de_Beer" title="Gavin de Beer">Gavin de Beer</a> beginning publication of Darwin's manuscripts including his private notebooks, and a new generation of science historians began investigating the Darwin papers archived at Cambridge, starting the "<a href="/wiki/Darwin_Industry" title="Darwin Industry">Darwin Industry</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDesmondMooreBrowne2007114–115_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDesmondMooreBrowne2007114–115-199">[199]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Darwin_College,_Cambridge" title="Darwin College, Cambridge">Darwin College</a>, a postgraduate college at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cambridge_University" title="Cambridge University">Cambridge University</a> founded in 1964, is named after the Darwin family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> From 2000 to 2017, UK £10 banknotes issued by the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England_note_issues" title="Bank of England note issues">Bank of England</a> featured Darwin's portrait printed on the reverse,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_noteworthy_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_noteworthy-201">[201]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CBBC_2013_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-CBBC_2013-202">[202]</a></sup> along with a <a href="/wiki/Hummingbird" title="Hummingbird">hummingbird</a> and <a href="/wiki/HMS_Beagle" title="HMS Beagle">HMS <i>Beagle</i></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bankofengland.co.uk_2005_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-bankofengland.co.uk_2005-203">[203]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2009 worldwide events, programmes and publications celebrated the bicentenary of Darwin's birth and the 150th anniversary of the publication of <i>On the Origin of Species</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> Marking his bicentenary, a statue of Darwin as a young man was placed in a courtyard of his <a href="/wiki/Alma_mater" title="Alma mater">alma mater</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christ%27s_College,_Cambridge" title="Christ's College, Cambridge">Christ's College, Cambridge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Darwin_statue_2009_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Darwin_statue_2009-37">[37]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Darwin_Day" title="Darwin Day">Darwin Day</a> became an annual celebration. Darwin was also commemorated on a <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain_commemorative_stamps_2000%E2%80%932009" title="Great Britain commemorative stamps 2000–2009">series of UK postage stamps</a> issued by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Mail" title="Royal Mail">Royal Mail</a> in 2009 with six "jigsaw" shaped stamps symbolising how his studies of different disciplines formed his new ideas on evolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Beagle:_In_Darwin%27s_wake" title="Beagle: In Darwin's wake">Beagle: In Darwin's wake</a></i> was a <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_the_Netherlands" title="Kingdom of the Netherlands">Dutch</a>-<a href="/wiki/Flanders" title="Flanders">Flemish</a> television series from 2009 and 2010 initiated by the <a href="/wiki/VPRO" title="VPRO">VPRO</a> in collaboration with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teleac_(broadcaster)" title="Teleac (broadcaster)">Teleac</a> and <a href="/wiki/Canvas_(TV_channel)" title="Canvas (TV channel)">Canvas</a> to commemorate the 150th anniversary of Darwin's <i>On the Origin of Species</i>. The series features an 8-month voyage around the world (commenced on 1 September 2009) on board the clipper <i><a href="/wiki/Stad_Amsterdam" title="Stad Amsterdam">Stad Amsterdam</a></i>, which follows the route of the <a href="/wiki/Second_voyage_of_HMS_Beagle" title="Second voyage of HMS Beagle">five-year-long voyage</a> of Darwin between 1831 and 1836. Biologist <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Darwin" title="Sarah Darwin">Sarah Darwin</a> (the great-great-granddaughter of Charles Darwin) is one of the recurring shipmates who appear in the series.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Children">Children</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Darwin%E2%80%93Wedgwood_family#Charles_Darwin" title="Darwin–Wedgwood family">Darwin–Wedgwood family § Charles Darwin</a></div>
<table class="toccolours" style="float:right;clear:right;font-size:85%;width:40%;margin-left:2em;">
<tbody><tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/William_Erasmus_Darwin" title="William Erasmus Darwin">William Erasmus Darwin</a></td>
<td style="text-align:right;">27 Dec 1839 –</td>
<td>8 Sept 1914
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Anne_Darwin" title="Anne Darwin">Anne Elizabeth Darwin</a></td>
<td style="text-align:right;">2 Mar 1841 –</td>
<td>23 Apr 1851
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Mary Eleanor Darwin</td>
<td style="text-align:right;">23 Sept 1842–</td>
<td>16 Oct 1842
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Henrietta_Litchfield" title="Henrietta Litchfield">Henrietta Emma Darwin</a></td>
<td style="text-align:right;">25 Sept 1843 –</td>
<td>17 Dec 1927
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/George_Darwin" title="George Darwin">George Howard Darwin</a></td>
<td style="text-align:right;">9 Jul 1845 –</td>
<td>7 Dec 1912
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Elizabeth Darwin</td>
<td style="text-align:right;">8 Jul 1847 –</td>
<td>8 Jun 1926
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Francis_Darwin" title="Francis Darwin">Francis Darwin</a></td>
<td style="text-align:right;">16 Aug 1848 –</td>
<td>19 Sept 1925
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Leonard_Darwin" title="Leonard Darwin">Leonard Darwin</a></td>
<td style="text-align:right;">15 Jan 1850 –</td>
<td>26 Mar 1943
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Horace_Darwin" title="Horace Darwin">Horace Darwin</a></td>
<td style="text-align:right;">13 May 1851 –</td>
<td>29 Sept 1928
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Charles Waring Darwin</td>
<td style="text-align:right;">6 Dec 1856 –</td>
<td>28 Jun 1858
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The Darwins had ten children: two died in infancy, and <a href="/wiki/Anne_Darwin" title="Anne Darwin">Annie</a>'s death at the age of ten had a devastating effect on her parents. Charles was a devoted father and uncommonly attentive to his children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whowas_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-whowas-13">[13]</a></sup> Whenever they fell ill, he feared that they might have inherited weaknesses from <a href="/wiki/Inbreeding" title="Inbreeding">inbreeding</a> due to the close family ties he shared with his <a href="/wiki/Cousin_marriage" title="Cousin marriage">wife and cousin</a>, Emma Wedgwood.
</p><p>He examined inbreeding in his writings, contrasting it with the advantages of <a href="/wiki/Outcrossing" title="Outcrossing">outcrossing</a> in many species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDesmondMoore1991447_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDesmondMoore1991447-208">[208]</a></sup> Despite his fears, most of the surviving children and many of their descendants went on to have distinguished careers.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:127px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Emma_Darwin_with_Charles_Waring_Darwin.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1512" data-file-width="999" decoding="async" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Emma_Darwin_with_Charles_Waring_Darwin.jpg/125px-Emma_Darwin_with_Charles_Waring_Darwin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Emma_Darwin_with_Charles_Waring_Darwin.jpg/188px-Emma_Darwin_with_Charles_Waring_Darwin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Emma_Darwin_with_Charles_Waring_Darwin.jpg/250px-Emma_Darwin_with_Charles_Waring_Darwin.jpg 2x" width="125"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Emma_Darwin_with_Charles_Waring_Darwin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Emma Darwin with Charles Waring Darwin</div></div></div>
<p>Charles Waring Darwin, born in December 1856, was the tenth and last of the children. Emma Darwin was aged 48 at the time of the birth, and the child was mentally subnormal and never learnt to walk or talk. He probably had <a href="/wiki/Down_syndrome" title="Down syndrome">Down syndrome</a>, which had not then been medically described. The evidence is a photograph by William Erasmus Darwin of the infant and his mother, showing a characteristic head shape, and the family's observations of the child.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup> Charles Waring died of <a href="/wiki/Scarlet_fever" title="Scarlet fever">scarlet fever</a> on 28 June 1858,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup> when Darwin wrote in his journal "Poor dear Baby died."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup> The death kept Darwin from attending the first <a href="/wiki/Publication_of_Darwin%27s_theory" title="Publication of Darwin's theory">publication of his theory</a> at the Linnean Society meeting on 1 July 1858. In <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Origin_of_Species" title="On the Origin of Species">On the Origin of Species</a></i>, Darwin spells out concerns about <a href="/wiki/Consanguinity" title="Consanguinity">consanguinity</a> and the "evil" effects of inbreeding.
</p><p>Of his surviving children, <a href="/wiki/George_Darwin" title="George Darwin">George</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Darwin" title="Francis Darwin">Francis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Horace_Darwin" title="Horace Darwin">Horace</a> became <a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Fellow of the Royal Society">Fellows of the Royal Society</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup> distinguished as astronomer,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> botanist and civil engineer, respectively. All three were knighted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> Another son, <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Darwin" title="Leonard Darwin">Leonard</a>, went on to be a soldier, politician, economist, <a href="/wiki/Eugenics" title="Eugenics">eugenicist</a> and mentor of the statistician and evolutionary biologist <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Fisher" title="Ronald Fisher">Ronald Fisher</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Views_and_opinions">Views and opinions</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_views">Religious views</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Religious_views_of_Charles_Darwin" title="Religious views of Charles Darwin">Religious views of Charles Darwin</a></div>
<p>Darwin's family tradition was <a href="/wiki/Nonconformist_(Protestantism)" title="Nonconformist (Protestantism)">nonconformist</a> <a href="/wiki/Unitarianism" title="Unitarianism">Unitarianism</a>, while his father and grandfather were <a href="/wiki/Freethought" title="Freethought">freethinkers</a>, and his <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boarding_school" title="Boarding school">boarding school</a> were <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-skool_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-skool-29">[29]</a></sup> When going to Cambridge to become an <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglican</a> clergyman, he did not "in the least doubt the strict and <a href="/wiki/Biblical_inerrancy" title="Biblical inerrancy">literal truth</a> of every word in the Bible".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dar57_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-dar57-41">[41]</a></sup> He learned <a href="/wiki/John_Herschel" title="John Herschel">John Herschel</a>'s science which, like <a href="/wiki/William_Paley" title="William Paley">William Paley</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Natural_theology" title="Natural theology">natural theology</a>, sought explanations in laws of nature rather than miracles and saw <a href="/wiki/Adaptation" title="Adaptation">adaptation</a> of species as <a href="/wiki/Teleological_argument" title="Teleological argument">evidence of design</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-syd5-7_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-syd5-7-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-db_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-db-44">[44]</a></sup> On board HMS <i>Beagle</i>, Darwin was quite <a href="/wiki/Orthodoxy" title="Orthodoxy">orthodox</a> and would quote the Bible as an authority on <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">morality</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biorelig_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-biorelig-216">[216]</a></sup> He looked for "centres of creation" to explain distribution,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-k356_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-k356-68">[68]</a></sup> and suggested that the very similar <a href="/wiki/Antlion" title="Antlion">antlions</a> found in Australia and England were evidence of a divine hand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crows_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Crows-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Annie_Darwin.jpg"><img alt="Three quarter length studio photo of seated girl about nine years old, looking slightly plump and rather solemn, in a striped dress, holding a basket of flowers on her lap." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1008" data-file-width="815" decoding="async" height="272" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Annie_Darwin.jpg/220px-Annie_Darwin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Annie_Darwin.jpg/330px-Annie_Darwin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Annie_Darwin.jpg/440px-Annie_Darwin.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Annie_Darwin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In 1851 Darwin was devastated when his daughter <a href="/wiki/Anne_Darwin" title="Anne Darwin">Annie</a> died. By then his faith in Christianity had dwindled, and he had stopped going to church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jvw41_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-jvw41-217">[217]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>By his return, he was <a href="/wiki/Historical_criticism" title="Historical criticism">critical of the Bible as history</a>, and wondered why all religions should not be equally valid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biorelig_216-1"><a href="#cite_note-biorelig-216">[216]</a></sup> In the next few years, while intensively speculating on geology and the <a href="/wiki/Transmutation_of_species" title="Transmutation of species">transmutation of species</a>, he gave much thought to religion and openly discussed this with his wife <a href="/wiki/Emma_Darwin" title="Emma Darwin">Emma</a>, whose beliefs similarly came from intensive study and questioning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Belief_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-Belief-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Theodicy" title="Theodicy">theodicy</a> of Paley and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomas_Malthus" title="Thomas Malthus">Thomas Malthus</a> vindicated evils such as starvation as a result of a benevolent creator's laws, which had an overall good effect. To Darwin, natural selection produced the good of adaptation but removed the need for design,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup> and he could not see the work of an omnipotent deity in all the pain and suffering, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ichneumon_wasp" title="Ichneumon wasp">ichneumon wasp</a> paralysing <a href="/wiki/Caterpillar" title="Caterpillar">caterpillars</a> as live food for its eggs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-miles_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-miles-160">[160]</a></sup> Though he thought of religion as a <a href="/wiki/Tribe" title="Tribe">tribal</a> survival strategy, Darwin was reluctant to give up the idea of <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">God as an ultimate lawgiver</a>. He was increasingly troubled by the <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_evil" title="Problem of evil">problem of evil</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup>
</p><p>Darwin remained close friends with the <a href="/wiki/Vicar_(Anglicanism)" title="Vicar (Anglicanism)">vicar</a> of Downe, <a href="/wiki/John_Brodie_Innes" title="John Brodie Innes">John Brodie Innes</a>, and continued to play a leading part in the parish work of the church,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup> but from around 1849 would go for a walk on Sundays while his family attended church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jvw41_217-1"><a href="#cite_note-jvw41-217">[217]</a></sup> He considered it "absurd to doubt that a man might be an ardent theist and an evolutionist"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fordyce_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fordyce-222">[222]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spencer_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-spencer-223">[223]</a></sup> and, though reticent about his religious views, in 1879 he wrote that "I have never been an atheist in the sense of denying the existence of a God. – I think that generally ... an agnostic would be the most correct description of my state of mind".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Belief_111-2"><a href="#cite_note-Belief-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fordyce_222-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fordyce-222">[222]</a></sup>
</p><p>The "<a href="/wiki/Elizabeth,_Lady_Hope" title="Elizabeth, Lady Hope">Lady Hope Story</a>", published in 1915, claimed that Darwin had reverted to Christianity on his sickbed. The claims were repudiated by Darwin's children and have been dismissed as false by historians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_society">Human society</span></h3>
<p>Darwin's views on social and political issues reflected his time and social position. He grew up in a family of <a href="/wiki/Whigs_(British_political_party)" title="Whigs (British political party)">Whig</a> reformers who, like his uncle Josiah Wedgwood, supported <a href="/wiki/Reform_Act_1832" title="Reform Act 1832">electoral reform</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Abolitionism in the United Kingdom">emancipation of slaves</a>. Darwin was passionately opposed to slavery, while seeing no problem with the working conditions of English factory workers or servants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrowne1995196–197_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrowne1995196–197-225">[225]</a></sup>
</p><p>Taking taxidermy lessons in 1826 from the freed slave <a href="/wiki/John_Edmonstone" title="John Edmonstone">John Edmonstone</a>, whom Darwin long recalled as "a very pleasant and intelligent man", reinforced his belief that black people shared the same feelings, and could be as intelligent as people of other races. He took the same attitude to native people he met on the <i>Beagle</i> voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrowne199566,_198,_240_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrowne199566,_198,_240-226">[226]</a></sup> Though commonplace in Britain at the time, <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Silliman" title="Benjamin Silliman">Silliman</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Bachman" title="John Bachman">Bachman</a> noticed the contast with slave-owning America. Around twenty years later racism became a feature of British society,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eddy_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-eddy-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Silliman,_Bachman_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-Silliman,_Bachman-227">[227]</a></sup> but Darwin remained strongly against slavery, against "ranking the so-called races of man as distinct species", and against ill-treatment of native people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="ref_Gnone"><a href="#endnote_Gnone">[VII]</a></sup>
</p><p>Darwin's interaction with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yaghan_people" title="Yaghan people">Yaghans</a> (Fuegians) such as <a href="/wiki/Jemmy_Button" title="Jemmy Button">Jemmy Button</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Second_voyage_of_HMS_Beagle" title="Second voyage of HMS Beagle">second voyage of HMS <i>Beagle</i></a> had a profound impact on his view of indigenous peoples. At his arrival to <a href="/wiki/Tierra_del_Fuego" title="Tierra del Fuego">Tierra del Fuego</a> he made a colourful description of "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fuegian" title="Fuegian">Fuegian</a> savages".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rozzi2018_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rozzi2018-229">[229]</a></sup> This view changed as he came to know Yaghan people more in detail. By studying the Yaghans, Darwin concluded that a number of basic emotions by different human groups were the same and that mental capabilities were roughly the same as for Europeans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rozzi2018_229-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rozzi2018-229">[229]</a></sup> While interested in Yaghan culture Darwin failed to appreciate their deep ecological knowledge and elaborate cosmology until the 1850s when he inspected a dictionary of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yaghan_language" title="Yaghan language">Yaghan</a> detailing 32,000 words.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rozzi2018_229-2"><a href="#cite_note-Rozzi2018-229">[229]</a></sup> He saw that European colonisation would often lead to the extinction of native civilisations, and "tr[ied] to integrate colonialism into an evolutionary history of civilization analogous to natural history".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barta_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-barta-230">[230]</a></sup>
</p><p>He thought men's eminence over women was the outcome of sexual selection, a view disputed by <a href="/wiki/Antoinette_Brown_Blackwell" title="Antoinette Brown Blackwell">Antoinette Brown Blackwell</a> in her 1875 book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sexes_Throughout_Nature" title="The Sexes Throughout Nature">The Sexes Throughout Nature</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vandermassen_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vandermassen-231">[231]</a></sup>
</p><p>Darwin was intrigued by his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Half-cousin" title="Half-cousin">half-cousin</a> <a href="/wiki/Francis_Galton" title="Francis Galton">Francis Galton</a>'s argument, introduced in 1865, that <a href="/wiki/Historiometry" title="Historiometry">statistical analysis</a> of <a href="/wiki/Heredity" title="Heredity">heredity</a> showed that moral and mental human traits could be inherited, and principles of animal breeding could apply to humans. In <i>The Descent of Man</i>, Darwin noted that aiding the weak to survive and have families could lose the benefits of natural selection, but cautioned that withholding such aid would endanger the instinct of sympathy, "the noblest part of our nature", and factors such as education could be more important. When Galton suggested that publishing research could encourage intermarriage within a "caste" of "those who are naturally gifted", Darwin foresaw practical difficulties, and thought it "the sole feasible, yet I fear <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Utopian" title="Utopian">utopian</a>, plan of procedure in improving the human race", preferring to simply publicise the importance of inheritance and leave decisions to individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup> Francis Galton named this field of study "<a href="/wiki/Eugenics" title="Eugenics">eugenics</a>" in 1883,<sup class="reference" id="ref_H1"><a href="#endnote_H1">[VIII]</a></sup> after Darwin's death, and his theories were cited to promote eugenic policies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barta_230-1"><a href="#cite_note-barta-230">[230]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionary_social_movements">Evolutionary social movements</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Darwinism" title="Darwinism">Darwinism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eugenics" title="Eugenics">Eugenics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Social_Darwinism" title="Social Darwinism">Social Darwinism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:VanityFair-Darwin2.jpg"><img alt="Full length portrait of a very thin white bearded Darwin, seated but leaning eagerly forward and smiling." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2383" data-file-width="1291" decoding="async" height="314" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/VanityFair-Darwin2.jpg/170px-VanityFair-Darwin2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/VanityFair-Darwin2.jpg/255px-VanityFair-Darwin2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/VanityFair-Darwin2.jpg/340px-VanityFair-Darwin2.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:VanityFair-Darwin2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Caricatures_of_Charles_Darwin_and_his_evolutionary_theory_in_19th-century_England" title="Caricatures of Charles Darwin and his evolutionary theory in 19th-century England">caricature of Darwin</a> from a 1871 <i><a href="/wiki/Vanity_Fair_(British_magazine)" title="Vanity Fair (British magazine)">Vanity Fair</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>Darwin's fame and popularity led to his name being associated with ideas and movements that, at times, had only an indirect relation to his writings, and sometimes went directly against his express comments.
</p><p>Thomas Malthus had argued that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malthusian_catastrophe" title="Malthusian catastrophe">population growth beyond resources</a> was ordained by God to get humans to <a href="/wiki/Protestant_work_ethic" title="Protestant work ethic">work productively</a> and show restraint in getting families; this was used in the 1830s to justify <a href="/wiki/Workhouse" title="Workhouse">workhouses</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laissez-faire_economics" title="Laissez-faire economics">laissez-faire economics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wm_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-wm-233">[233]</a></sup> Evolution was by then seen as having social implications, and <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Spencer" title="Herbert Spencer">Herbert Spencer</a>'s 1851 book <i>Social Statics</i> based ideas of human freedom and individual liberties on his Lamarckian evolutionary theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup>
</p><p>Soon after the <i>Origin</i> was published in 1859, critics derided his description of a struggle for existence as a Malthusian justification for the English industrial capitalism of the time. The term <i>Darwinism</i> was used for the evolutionary ideas of others, including Spencer's "<a href="/wiki/Survival_of_the_fittest" title="Survival of the fittest">survival of the fittest</a>" as free-market progress, and <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Haeckel" title="Ernst Haeckel">Ernst Haeckel</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Polygenism" title="Polygenism">polygenistic</a> ideas of <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Haeckel#Polygenism_and_racial_theory" title="Ernst Haeckel">human development</a>. Writers used natural selection to argue for various, often contradictory, ideologies such as laissez-faire dog-eat-dog capitalism, <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonialism</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Imperialism" title="New Imperialism">imperialism</a>. However, Darwin's holistic view of nature included "dependence of one being on another"; thus <a href="/wiki/Pacifism" title="Pacifism">pacifists</a>, socialists, liberal social reformers and anarchists such as <a href="/wiki/Peter_Kropotkin" title="Peter Kropotkin">Peter Kropotkin</a> stressed the value of co-operation over struggle within a species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup> Darwin himself insisted that social policy should not simply be guided by concepts of struggle and selection in nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the 1880s, a eugenics movement developed on ideas of biological inheritance, and for scientific justification of their ideas appealed to some concepts of Darwinism. In Britain, most shared Darwin's cautious views on voluntary improvement and sought to encourage those with good traits in "positive eugenics". During the "Eclipse of Darwinism", a scientific foundation for eugenics was provided by <a href="/wiki/Mendelian_inheritance" title="Mendelian inheritance">Mendelian</a> <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetics</a>. Negative eugenics to remove the "feebleminded" were popular in America, Canada and Australia, and <a href="/wiki/Eugenics_in_the_United_States" title="Eugenics in the United States">eugenics in the United States</a> introduced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compulsory_sterilisation" title="Compulsory sterilisation">compulsory sterilisation</a> laws, followed by several other countries. Subsequently, <a href="/wiki/Nazi_eugenics" title="Nazi eugenics">Nazi eugenics</a> brought the field into disrepute.<sup class="reference" id="ref_H2"><a href="#endnote_H2">[VIII]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term "<a href="/wiki/Social_Darwinism" title="Social Darwinism">Social Darwinism</a>" was used infrequently from around the 1890s, but became popular as a derogatory term in the 1940s when used by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Hofstadter" title="Richard Hofstadter">Richard Hofstadter</a> to attack the <a href="/wiki/Laissez-faire" title="Laissez-faire">laissez-faire</a> conservatism of those like <a href="/wiki/William_Graham_Sumner" title="William Graham Sumner">William Graham Sumner</a> who opposed reform and socialism. Since then, it has been used as a term of abuse by those opposed to what they think are the moral consequences of evolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wm_233-1"><a href="#cite_note-wm-233">[233]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Works">Works</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin_bibliography" title="Charles Darwin bibliography">Charles Darwin bibliography</a></div>
<p>Darwin was a prolific writer. Even without publication of his works on evolution, he would have had a considerable reputation as the author of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Voyage_of_the_Beagle" title="The Voyage of the Beagle">The Voyage of the Beagle</a></i>, as a geologist who had published extensively on South America and had solved the puzzle of the formation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coral_atoll" title="Coral atoll">coral atolls</a>, and as a biologist who had published the definitive work on <a href="/wiki/Barnacle" title="Barnacle">barnacles</a>. While <i>On the Origin of Species</i> dominates perceptions of his work, <i>The Descent of Man</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Expression_of_the_Emotions_in_Man_and_Animals" title="The Expression of the Emotions in Man and Animals">The Expression of the Emotions in Man and Animals</a></i> had considerable impact, and his books on plants including <i><a href="/wiki/The_Power_of_Movement_in_Plants" title="The Power of Movement in Plants">The Power of Movement in Plants</a></i> were innovative studies of great importance, as was his final work on <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Formation_of_Vegetable_Mould_through_the_Action_of_Worms" title="The Formation of Vegetable Mould through the Action of Worms">The Formation of Vegetable Mould through the Action of Worms</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1054258005">.mw-parser-output .refbegin{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul{margin-left:0}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul>li{margin-left:0;padding-left:3.2em;text-indent:-3.2em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents ul,.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents ul li{list-style:none}@media(max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul>li{padding-left:1.6em;text-indent:-1.6em}}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="refbegin" style="">
<p><b><span style="font-size:85%;">I</span>.</b> <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1041539562">.mw-parser-output .citation{word-wrap:break-word}.mw-parser-output .citation:target{background-color:rgba(0,127,255,0.133)}</style><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Anone"><a href="#ref_Anone"><b><sup>^</sup></b></a></span>  Darwin was eminent as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naturalist" title="Naturalist">naturalist</a>, geologist, <a href="/wiki/Biologist" title="Biologist">biologist</a>, and author. After a summer as a <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">physician</a>'s assistant (helping his father) and two years as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medical_student" title="Medical student">medical student</a>, he went to Cambridge for the ordinary degree to qualify as a clergyman; he was also trained in <a href="/wiki/Taxidermy" title="Taxidermy">taxidermy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODNB_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-240">[240]</a></sup>
</p><p><b><span style="font-size:85%;">II</span>.</b> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1041539562" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Bnone"><a href="#ref_Bnone"><b><sup>^</sup></b></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Robert_FitzRoy" title="Robert FitzRoy">Robert FitzRoy</a> was to become known after the voyage for <a href="/wiki/Biblical_literalism" title="Biblical literalism">biblical literalism</a>, but at this time he had considerable interest in Lyell's ideas, and they met before the voyage when Lyell asked for observations to be made in South America. FitzRoy's diary during the ascent of the River Santa Cruz in <a href="/wiki/Patagonia" title="Patagonia">Patagonia</a> recorded his opinion that the plains were <a href="/wiki/Raised_beach" title="Raised beach">raised beaches</a>, but on return, newly married to a very religious lady, he recanted these ideas.(<a href="#CITEREFBrowne1995">Browne 1995</a>, pp. 186, 414)
</p><p><b><span style="font-size:85%;">III</span>.</b> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1041539562" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Cnone"><a href="#ref_Cnone"><b><sup>^</sup></b></a></span>  In the section <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">"Morphology"</a> of Chapter XIII of <i>On the Origin of Species</i>, Darwin commented on <a href="/wiki/Homology_(biology)" title="Homology (biology)">homologous</a> bone patterns between humans and other mammals, writing: "What can be more curious than that the hand of a man, formed for grasping, that of a mole for digging, the leg of the horse, the paddle of the porpoise, and the wing of the bat, should all be constructed on the same pattern, and should include the same bones, in the same relative positions?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup> and in the concluding chapter: "The framework of bones being the same in the hand of a man, wing of a bat, fin of the porpoise, and leg of the horse … at once explain themselves on the theory of descent with slow and slight successive modifications."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[242]</a></sup>
</p><p><b><span style="font-size:85%;">IV</span>.</b> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1041539562" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_D1"><a href="#ref_D1"><b><sup>1</sup></b></a></span> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1041539562" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_D2"><a href="#ref_D2"><b><sup>2</sup></b></a></span> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1041539562" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_D3"><a href="#ref_D3"><b><sup>3</sup></b></a></span> 
In <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Origin_of_Species" title="On the Origin of Species">On the Origin of Species</a></i> Darwin mentioned <a href="/wiki/Human_evolution" title="Human evolution">human origins</a> in his concluding remark that "In the distant future I see open fields for far more important researches. Psychology will be based on a new foundation, that of the necessary acquirement of each mental power and capacity by gradation. Light will be thrown on the origin of man and his history."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-light_on_man_146-2"><a href="#cite_note-light_on_man-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>In "Chapter VI: Difficulties on Theory" he referred to <a href="/wiki/Sexual_selection" title="Sexual selection">sexual selection</a>: "I might have adduced for this same purpose the differences between the races of man, which are so strongly marked; I may add that some little light can apparently be thrown on the origin of these differences, chiefly through sexual selection of a particular kind, but without here entering on copious details my reasoning would appear frivolous."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SS_man_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-SS_man-145">[145]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <i><a href="/wiki/The_Descent_of_Man,_and_Selection_in_Relation_to_Sex" title="The Descent of Man, and Selection in Relation to Sex">The Descent of Man</a></i> of 1871, Darwin discussed the first passage:
"During many years I collected notes on the origin or descent of man, without any intention of publishing on the subject, but rather with the determination not to publish, as I thought that I should thus only add to the prejudices against my views. It seemed to me sufficient to indicate, in the first edition of my 'Origin of Species,' that by this work 'light would be thrown on the origin of man and his history;' and this implies that man must be included with other organic beings in any general conclusion respecting his manner of appearance on this earth."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[243]</a></sup> In a preface to the 1874 second edition, he added a reference to the second point: "it has been said by several critics, that when I found that many details of structure in man could not be explained through natural selection, I invented sexual selection; I gave, however, a tolerably clear sketch of this principle in the first edition of the 'Origin of Species,' and I there stated that it was applicable to man."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[244]</a></sup>
</p><p><b><span style="font-size:85%;">V</span>.</b> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1041539562" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Enone"><a href="#ref_Enone"><b><sup>^</sup></b></a></span>  See, for example, WILLA volume 4, <i><a class="external text" href="http://scholar.lib.vt.edu/ejournals/old-WILLA/fall95/DeSimone.html" rel="nofollow">Charlotte Perkins Gilman and the Feminization of Education</a></i> by Deborah M. De Simone: "Gilman shared many basic educational ideas with the generation of thinkers who matured during the period of "intellectual chaos" caused by Darwin's Origin of the Species. Marked by the belief that individuals can direct human and social evolution, many progressives came to view education as the panacea for advancing social progress and for solving such problems as urbanisation, poverty, or immigration."
</p><p><b><span style="font-size:85%;">VI</span>.</b> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1041539562" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Fnone"><a href="#ref_Fnone"><b><sup>^</sup></b></a></span>  See, for example, the song "A lady fair of lineage high" from <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_and_Sullivan" title="Gilbert and Sullivan">Gilbert and Sullivan</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Princess_Ida" title="Princess Ida">Princess Ida</a></i>, which describes the descent of man (but not woman!) from apes.
</p><p><b><span style="font-size:85%;">VII</span>.</b> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1041539562" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Gnone"><a href="#ref_Gnone"><b><sup>^</sup></b></a></span>  Darwin's belief that black people had the same essential humanity as Europeans, and had many mental similarities, was reinforced by the lessons he had from <a href="/wiki/John_Edmonstone" title="John Edmonstone">John Edmonstone</a> in 1826.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eddy_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-eddy-31">[31]</a></sup> Early in the <i>Beagle</i> voyage, Darwin nearly lost his position on the ship when he criticised FitzRoy's defence and praise of slavery. (<a href="#CITEREFDarwin1958">Darwin 1958</a>, p. <a class="external text" href="http://darwin-online.org.uk/content/frameset?viewtype=text&amp;itemID=F1497&amp;pageseq=76" rel="nofollow">74</a>) He wrote home about "how steadily the general feeling, as shown at elections, has been rising against Slavery. What a proud thing for England if she is the first European nation which utterly abolishes it! I was told before leaving England that after living in slave countries all my opinions would be altered; the only alteration I am aware of is forming a much higher estimate of the negro character." (<a href="#CITEREFDarwin1887">Darwin 1887</a>, p. <a class="external text" href="http://darwin-online.org.uk/content/frameset?viewtype=text&amp;itemID=F1452.1&amp;pageseq=264" rel="nofollow">246</a>) Regarding <a href="/wiki/Fuegians" title="Fuegians">Fuegians</a>, he "could not have believed how wide was the difference between savage and civilized man: it is greater than between a wild and domesticated animal, inasmuch as in man there is a greater power of improvement", but he knew and liked civilised Fuegians like <a href="/wiki/Jemmy_Button" title="Jemmy Button">Jemmy Button</a>: "It seems yet wonderful to me, when I think over all his many good qualities, that he should have been of the same race, and doubtless partaken of the same character, with the miserable, degraded savages whom we first met here."(<a href="#CITEREFDarwin1845">Darwin 1845</a>, pp. <a class="external text" href="http://darwin-online.org.uk/content/frameset?itemID=F14&amp;viewtype=text&amp;pageseq=218" rel="nofollow">205, 207–208</a>)
</p><p>In the <i>Descent of Man</i>, he mentioned the similarity of Fuegians' and Edmonstone's minds to Europeans' when arguing against "ranking the so-called races of man as distinct species".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[245]</a></sup>
</p><p>He rejected the ill-treatment of native people, and for example wrote of massacres of <a href="/wiki/Patagonia" title="Patagonia">Patagonian</a> men, women, and children, "Every one here is fully convinced that this is the most just war, because it is against barbarians. Who would believe in this age that such atrocities could be committed in a Christian civilized country?"(<a href="#CITEREFDarwin1845">Darwin 1845</a>, p. <a class="external text" href="http://darwin-online.org.uk/content/frameset?viewtype=text&amp;itemID=F14&amp;pageseq=115" rel="nofollow">102</a>)
</p><p><b><span style="font-size:85%;">VIII</span>.</b> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1041539562" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_H1"><a href="#ref_H1"><b><sup>1</sup></b></a></span> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1041539562" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_H2"><a href="#ref_H2"><b><sup>2</sup></b></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Geneticist" title="Geneticist">Geneticists</a> studied human heredity as <a href="/wiki/Mendelian_inheritance" title="Mendelian inheritance">Mendelian inheritance</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Eugenics" title="Eugenics">eugenics</a> movements sought to manage society, with a focus on social class in the United Kingdom, and on disability and ethnicity in the United States, leading to geneticists seeing this movement as impractical <a href="/wiki/Pseudoscience" title="Pseudoscience">pseudoscience</a>. A shift from voluntary arrangements to "negative" eugenics included <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compulsory_sterilisation" title="Compulsory sterilisation">compulsory sterilisation</a> laws in the United States, copied by <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> as the basis for <a href="/wiki/Nazi_eugenics" title="Nazi eugenics">Nazi eugenics</a> based on virulent racism and "<a href="/wiki/Racial_hygiene" title="Racial hygiene">racial hygiene</a>".<br/>(<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1067248974">.mw-parser-output </p><p><b><span style="font-size:85%;">IX</span>.</b> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1041539562" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Inone"><a href="#ref_Inone"><b><sup>^</sup></b></a></span> <a href="/wiki/David_Quammen" title="David Quammen">David Quammen</a> writes of his "theory that [Darwin] turned to these arcane botanical studies – producing more than one book that was solidly empirical, discreetly evolutionary, yet a 'horrid bore' – at least partly so that the clamorous controversialists, fighting about apes and angels and souls, would leave him... alone". <a href="/wiki/David_Quammen" title="David Quammen">David Quammen</a>, "The Brilliant Plodder" (review of Ken Thompson, <i>Darwin's Most Wonderful Plants: A Tour of His Botanical Legacy</i>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Chicago_Press" title="University of Chicago Press">University of Chicago Press</a>, 255 pp.; Elizabeth Hennessy, <i>On the Backs of Tortoises: Darwin, the Galápagos, and the Fate of an Evolutionary Eden</i>, <a href="/wiki/Yale_University_Press" title="Yale University Press">Yale University Press</a>, 310 pp.; Bill Jenkins, <i>Evolution Before Darwin: Theories of the Transmutation of Species in Edinburgh, 1804–1834</i>, <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh_University_Press" title="Edinburgh University Press">Edinburgh University Press</a>, 222 pp.), <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Review_of_Books" title="The New York Review of Books">The New York Review of Books</a></i>, vol. LXVII, no. 7 (23 April 2020), pp. 22–24. Quammen, quoted from p. 24 of his review.
</p>
</div>

<li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFCoyne2009"><a href="/wiki/Jerry_Coyne" title="Jerry Coyne">Coyne, Jerry A.</a> (2009). <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/isbn_9780199230846/page/17" rel="nofollow"><i>Why Evolution is True</i></a>. Much of that book presents evidence that not only supports evolution but at the same time refutes creationism. In Darwin's day, the evidence for his theories was compelling but not completely decisive.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Why+Evolution+is+True&amp;rft.place=Oxford&amp;rft.pages=17&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2009&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-19-923084-6&amp;rft.aulast=Coyne&amp;rft.aufirst=Jerry+A.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2Fisbn_9780199230846%2Fpage%2F17&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACharles+Darwin"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As Darwinian scholar Joseph Carroll of the University of Missouri–St. Louis puts it in his introduction to a modern reprint of Darwin's work: "<i>The Origin of Species</i> has special claims on our attention. It is one of the two or three most significant works of all time—one of those works that fundamentally and permanently alter our vision of the world...It is argued with a singularly rigorous consistency but it is also eloquent, imaginatively evocative, and rhetorically compelling." <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFCarroll,_Joseph2003">Carroll, Joseph, ed. (2003). <i>On the origin of species by means of natural selection</i>. Peterborough, Ontario: Broadview. p. 15. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-55111-337-1" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-55111-337-1"><bdi>978-1-55111-337-1</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=On+the+origin+of+species+by+means+of+natural+selection&amp;rft.place=Peterborough%2C+Ontario&amp;rft.pages=15&amp;rft.pub=Broadview&amp;rft.date=2003&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-55111-337-1&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACharles+Darwin"></span></span>
